LE PRIMER REport des Cases and Matters en Ley resolves and adiudges en les
Courts del Roy en Ireland.

Collect et digest per Sr. John Dauys Chiualer Attorney General del Roy en
cest Realm.

Liber librum aperit.

DVBLIN. Printed by John Franckton Printer to the Kings most excellent Majesty
Anno. 1615.

TO THE RIGHT HONORABLE MY SINGVLAR GOOD LORD, THOMAS LORD ELLESMERE LORD
CHAVNcellor of England.
KIng Henry the Second (my most honorable good Lord) was the first King of
England after the Norman Conquest, that was styled Lord of Ireland. Yet are
there no records of that kings time remaining, whereby it may appear, that he
established any form of Civil government in this land. But it is manifest by
many records, and stories, that his son King John made the first division of
Counties in Ireland, published the laws of England, and commanded the due
execution thereof in all those countries which he had made, erected the Courts
of Justice, made the Standard of Irish moneys equal with the English:
Breefely, he did order and settle the government here in all points,
according to the Model of the commonwealth of England. And to that end, when
himself in person came over into Ireland the second time (which was in the
twelfth year of his reign) he brought with him many learned persons in the
law, and other Officers, and ministers of all sorts, to put the English laws
in execution: whereof there is a notable record in the Tower of London, 11.
Henr. 3. Patent. Membr. 3. agreeing with that which is related by Matthew. Paris.
histor. magn. sol. 220 b.

After which time the records of all legal Acts and proceedings, namely the
Piperolls, containing the charge of the revenue both Certain and casual, the
Plearolls, containing as well Common pleas, as pleas of the Crown, Parliament
Rolls, Charters, Patents, Commissions, and Inquisitions, were made up in good
form in every kings time, till the later end of the reign of King Henry VI,
when by reason of the dissension of the two Royal houses, the state of England
neglecting the government of this Realm, the Clarks and Officers grew also
negligent in the execution of their several places. And though many of those
ancient records have been embezeled, and many have perished by carlesse
keeping, yet diverse of all sorts do yet remain, as fair and authentic, as
any I have seen in England. Howbeit during all the time that the laws of
England have had their course in Ireland, which is now full four hundred
years, there has not been any Report made and published of any Case in law,
argued, or adjudged in this Kingdom: but all the arguments and reasons of the
judgments and resolutions given in the Courts of Ireland, have hitherto been
utterly lost, and buried in oblivion.

Which seems to me the more strange, because there have been within this
Realm, in every age since the reign of King John, men sufficienly learned in
the laws, who have derived their learning out of the fountains of law in
England, the Innes of Court there (being the most flourishing and honorable
Academy of gentlemen, that ever was established in any nation, for the study
and learning of the Municipall laws thereof) And therefore they might have
been induced to imitate the learned men of England, who from the Norman
Conquest downwards, did continually preseiue the memory of such notable cases
as did from time to time arise, and where argued and ruled in the Courts of
Justice in England, by reducing the same into books of Reports, which may be
called, not improperly, the Annalles of the law.

For albeit our Reports at large which are published in Print, do beginn with
the reign of king Edw. III. And the broken Cases of elder times, which are
scattered in the Abridgements, are not found higher then the time of king Henry
III. yet assuredly there were other Reports digested in years and Terms, as
ancient as the time of king William the Conqueror: as appears by that which
Chaucer writteth of the Seriaunt at law In Terms had he Cases and Doomes all,
That fro that time of King William were fall.


Neither does Glanuill or Bracton disaffirme this antiquity of the Reports of
the law, in that they affirm that the law of England was Ius non scriptum in
their times, as your Lordship has noted in that most learned, grave, and
prudent speech of yours, touching the Postnati of Scotland. For indeed those
Reports are but Comments or interpretations upon the Text of the Common law:
which Text was never originally written, but has ever bin preserved in the
memory of men, though no man's memory can reach to the original thereof.

For the Common law of England is nothing else but the Common custom of the
Realm: And a custom which has obtained the force of a law, is always said
to be Ius non scriptum, for it cannot be made or created, either by Charter,
or by Parliament, which are acts reduced to writting, and are always matter
of Record, but being only matter of fact and consisting in use and practice,
it can be recorded and registered no where, but in the memory of the people.

For a Custom takes beginning and grows to perfection in this manner. When
a reasonable act once done, is found to be good and beneficial to the people,
and agreeable to their nature and disposition, then do they use it, and
practice it, again, and again, and so by often iteration and multiplication
of the act, it becomes a Custom, and being continued without interruption
time out of mind, it obtains the force of a law.

And this Custumary law is the most perfect, and most excellent, and without
comparison the best, to make and preserve a commonwealth, for the written laws
which are made either by the edicts of Princes, or by Counselles of estate, are
imposed upon the subject before any Trial or Probation made, whether the same
be fit and agreeable to the nature and disposition of the people, or whether
they will breed any inconvenience or no. But a Custom does never become a law
to bind the people, until it has bin tried and approved time out of mind,
during all which time there did thereby arise no inconvenience, for if it had
been found inconvenient at any time, it had been used no longer, but had
been interrupted, and consequently it had lost the virtue and force of a law.

Therefore as the law of nature, which the schoolmen call Ius commune, and
which is also Ius non scriptum, being written only in the heart of man, is
better then all the written laws in the world to make men honest and happy in
this life, if they would observe the rules thereof: So the custumary law of
England, which we do likewise call Ius commune as coming nearest to the law
of Nature, which is the root and touchstone of all good laws, and which is
also Ius non scriptum, and written only in the memory of man (for every
custom though it took beginning beyond the memory of any living man, yet it
is continued and preserved in the memory of men living) does far excel our
written laws, namely our Statutes or Acts of Parliament: which is manifest in
this, that when our Parliaments have altered or changed any fundamental
points of the Common law, those alterations have been found by experience to
be so inconvenient for the commonwealth, as that the common law has in
effect been restored again, in the same points, by other Acts of Parliament,
in suceeding ages.

And as our Custumary unwritten law does excel our Parliament laws, which
are written, so for the government of the Commonweal of England (which is as
well instituted and established as any Commonweal in Christendom.) Our
native Common law is far more apt and agreeable, then the Civil or Canon
law, or any other written law in the world besides: howsoever some of our
own Countrymen, who are Ciues in aliena Republica, and hospites in sua, may
per^^ppes affirm the contrary. But certain it is, That the great and
wisemen of England in the Parliament of Merton did not prefer a Foreign
law before their own, when motion being made by the Clergy that Children
borne before Marriage might be adjudged legitumate, They all made answer with
one voice, Nolumus Leges Angliae mutari, And again in II. R. 2. when a new
course of proceeding in Criminal Causes, according to the form of the Civil
law was propounded in that unruly Parliament, Answer was made by all the
Estates, That the Realm of England, neither had bin in former times, nor
hereafter should be Ruled and governed by the Civil law. Rot Parliam II. R.
2. in Archiv. Turris.

And here I may observe for the Honor of our Nation, and of our Ancestors
who have founded this Commonweal wherein we live, and enjoy so many
felicities, That England having had a good and happy Genius from the
beginning, has bin enhabited always with a virtuous and wise people, who ever
embraced honest and good Customs, full of Reason and conveniencie, which being
confirmed by common use and practice, and continued time out of mind, became
the common law of the Land. And though this law be the peculiar invention of
this Nation, and delivered over from age to age by Tradition (for the common
law of England is a Tradition, and learned by Tradition as well as by Books)
yet may we truly say, That no human law written or unwritten has more
certainty in the Rules and Maxims, more coherence in the parts thereof, or
more harmony of reason in it: nay we may confidently averr, that it does
excel all other laws in upholding a free Monarchy, which is the most
excellent form of government, exalting the prerogative Royal, and being very
tender and watchful to preserve it, and yet maintaining withal the ingenuous
liberty of the subject.

Breefely, it is ^o framed and sitted to the nature and disposition of this
people, as we may properly say, it is connaturall to the Nation, so as it
cannot possibly be ruled by any other law. This law therefore does
demonstrate the strength of wit and reason, and self sufficiency which has
been always in the people of this land, which have made their own laws out
of their wisdom and experience (like a silk worm that forms all her webb
out of her self only) not begging or borrowing a form of a commonweal
either from Rome, or from Greece, as all other nations of Europe have done, but
having sufficient provision of law and Justice within the land, have no need
Iustitiam and iudicium a^ alienigenis emendicare, as King John wrote most nobly
to Pope Innocent the third. Matthew Parishistor. magn page 215. En populus sapiens
and intelligens, gens magna: As it is said of Gods chosen people 4. Deuter.
Neither could any one man ever vaunt, that, like Minos, Solon, or Lycurgus, he
was the first Lawegiuer to our Nation: for neither did the King make his own
prerogative, nor the Judges make the Rules or Maxims of the law, nor the
common subject prescribe and limitt the liberties which he enjoys by the
law: but as it is said of every Art or Science which is brought to perfection,
Per varios vsus artem experientia fecit, so may it properly be said of our
law, Per varios vsus legem experientia fecit. Long experience, and many
trialles of what was best for the common good, did make the Common law.

But upon what reason then does Polidor Virgill and other writers affirm,
that King William the Conqueror was our Lawegiuer, and caused all our laws to
be written in French? Assuredly, the Norman Conqueror found the ancient laws
of England so honorable, and profitable, both for the Prince and people, as
that he thought it not fit to make any alteration in the fundamental points
or substance thereof: the change that was made was but in formulis iuris: he
altered some legal forms of proceeding, and to honor his own language, and
for a mark of Conquest withal, he caused the pleading of diverse Actions to be
made and entered in French, and set forth his public Ordinances and Acts of
Counsel in the same tongue: which form of pleading in French continued till
36. Edw. 3. when (in regard that the French tongue begann to grow out of use,
which for many years after the Norman Conquest was as common as the English
among the Gentry of England) it was ordained by Parliament, that all pleas
should be pleaded, debated, and Judged in the English tongue, and entered, and
enrolled in Latin. And as for our statutes or Acts of Parliament, the bills
were for the most part exhibited in French, and passed and enrolled in the same
language, even till the time of King H. 7. And so are they printed in Rastalles
first Abridgment of statutes published in the year 1559. But after the
beginning of King Henry 7. his reign, we find all our Acts of Parliament
recorded in English. Only our Reports of the Cases, resolutions, and Judgments
in the law, whereof our books of the law do consist, have ever until this
day been penned and published in that mixed kind of speech which we call the
law French, differing indeed not a little from the French tongue, as it is now
refined and spoken in France, as well by reason of the words of Art and form,
called the Terms of the law, as for that we do still retain many other
old words and Phrases of speech which were used four hundred years since,
and are now become obsolete and out of use among them, but are grown by long
and continual use so apt, so natural, and so proper for the matter and
subject of these Reports, as no other language is significant enough to
express the same, but only this law french wherein they are written.

And this is the true and only cause, why our Reports and other books of the
law for the most part, are not set forth in English, Latin, or the modern
french, for that the proper and peculiar phrase of the common law cannot be
so well expressed, nor any case in law be so succinctly, sensibly, and whithall
so fully reported, as in this speech, which is in deed mixte and compounded of
all these three languages. Which reason has not been well understood by
those, who object it as a fault to the Professors of our law, that, forsooth,
they write their Reports and books of the law in a strange unknown tongue
which none can understand but themselves, to the end, that the people, being
keept in ignorance of the law, may the more admire their skill and knowledge,
and esteem and value it at a higher price. As Cicero in his first book de
Oratore does testify, that the like conceit was held of the first Professors
of the Civil law, Quia veteres illi qui huic scientiae praesuerunt,
obtinendae atque augendae potentiae suae causa, pervulgari artem suam
no^uerunt. And Caesar speaking of the Druides, who were Judges, and interpreters
of the law among the ancient Britains, does report of them, that though
they spent twenty years in the study of those laws, Non existimabant fas esse
^a literis mandare.

But the weakness of this objection against the Authors of our law books will
easily appear, if we consider how easy the Law french is to be learned: in
so much that the meanest wit that ever came to the study of the law, does come
to understand it almost perfectly within ten days without a Reader. So as we
do not seal or lock up the mysteries of our law in Hieroglyphickes, or in a
dark language that cannot be understood: But we express the Cases,
arguments, and Judgments of the law in a form of speech, so plain, so
significant, and in a tongue so soon learned by any man, that can speak
English, and understand Latin, as I dare say, there is no rational science in
the world, having so many words and Terms of art and form, that is so
clearly delivered in any language. And I may truly say withal, that if the
books of our law were all translated into English, they would not be better,
nay, they would not be so well understood by the students thereof, as in this
proper and peculiar language wherein they are now written.

And as this objection touching the speech or language wherein our Reports are
penned does arise out of ignorance of the cause thereof, as is before declared,
so are there other vulgar imputations cast upon the law and lawiers, which
may be as easily cleared, as having indeed no other ground but the mere
misunderstanding of such as are strangers to the profession: namely 1. that
there is much uncertainty in the reasons and Judgments of the law. 2. that
there are extreme and unnecessary delays in the proceedings of the law.
(...). that many bad and dishonest causes are wittingly defended by the
professors of the law. But Sapientia iustificatur a filijs suis.

1 Therefore first touching the incerteinty of the law* Certain it is, that
law is nothing but a rule of reason, and human reason is Lesbia regula,
pliable every way, or like a cupp with two ears, as the French proue^b is,
which may be taken up on either side, as well with the left hand as with the
right: so as not only the knowledge of the law, but all other rational
sciences that are subject to Argument and discourse, must needs be subject to
uncertainty and to error: and therefore upon Judgments given in our Ordinary
Courts of Justice the law does admit and allow writts of error to be
brought, without any touch or dishonor to the Judges, though there Judgments
be reuersed for error in point of law.

Howbeit there is no art or science that stands upon discourse of reason,
that has her Rules and Maxims so certain and infallible, and so little
subject to diverse interpretation as the common law of England, as it is
observed by the Lord chief Justice Cook in his Preface to the second part of
his Reports, that in all his time, there have not been moved in the Courts of
Justice in England, two quest^ons touching the right of discents or escheats,
or the like fundamental points of the common law: So certain, sure, and
without question are the principles and grounds thereof.

But whence then do so many debates and controuersis arise? whereupon do we
plead, and contend so much in the Courts of Justice, it there be so few
doubts and vncerteinties in law? doubtless this question is soon resolved,
by one plain and common distinction. In all the causes that are controuerted,
there is either Quastio Iuris, or quaestio facti. But for one cause wherein a
question of law does arise, that is indeed with the debating, there are a
thousand causes at least wherein the fact is only in question, and wherein if
the truth of the fact were known, the law were clear and without question.
So as the pleading and contention in Westminster hall, and the rest of the
Courts of Justice in both realms, is for the most part touching matters of
fact. In the Chauncery whether there be Trust or no trust, Fraud or no fraud:
In the Starchamber whither a Riot^ or no riott, Forgery or no forgery. Per^u^y
or no periu^y: and the like matters of fact come only in question in all other
Courts which proceed to the hearing and determining of causes by examination of
witnesses. And in the Co^^^^ of law where the trial is by Jurors, are there
not a thousand issues joined upon matters of fact, for one demurrer that is
joined upon a point in law? and when all these issues are tried either at the
Bar or at the A^s^sses, how many hundreds of general verdictes are there
given which determine matters in fact, for one special verdict whereupon do
result questions in law?

And again of all the questions in law which do arise upon demurrers or
special verdicts, or which are moved in arrest of Judgment, how many of them
are there over ruled upon the first opening or putting of the Case? and how
few of them are there that are malleable or can endure the hammer, so as they
come to be solemnly argued at the Bar, and at the Bench? As for the
Eschequer-chamber cases which are of such difficulty as that they draw an
assembly of all the Judges of the law for the resolution thereof, they are so
rare as scarce twice in a year are those Judges drawn out of there proper
Courts to deliver their opinions upon those doubtful points. So as it is
to be ascribed to the great learning, wisdom, gravity, and constancy of our
Judges, and to the certeinty and excellent harmony of reason in our law, that
there are no more diversities of opinion among the Judges, or doubtful
questions in the law then there are.

For if the Rules and Maxims of the law were a thousand times as many as they
be indeed, yet would they carry no proportion with the infinite diversity of
men's actions, and of other accidents which make the cases that are to be
decided by the law. Besides, it must be a work of singular Judgment, to
apply the grounds and rules of the law which are fixed and certain, to all
human acts and accidents which are in perpetual motion and mutation.

And therefore we may truly say for the honor of our law, notwithstanding
that vulgar imputation of incerteinty, that the Judgment and reason of it is
more certain then of any other humane law in the world: As well because the
grounds of our common law have from the beginning been laid with such deep
wisdom, policy, and providence, as that they do provide for, and meet with,
almost all cases that can possibly fall out in our commonwealth, as also
because those grounds are so plain, and so clear, as that the professors of
our law have not thought it needful to make so many glosses and
interpretations therevppon, as other laws are perplexed and confounded
withal: which glosses, as one does well observe, do increase doubt and
ignorance in all Arts and Sciences. And therefore the Ciuilians themselves
confess, that their law is a sea full of waves, the Text whereof being
digested into so many volumes, and so many Doctors interpreting the Text, and
twice as many more Commenting upon their interpretations, and so gloss upon
gloss, and book upon book, and every Doctors opinion being a good authority
fit to be cited and vouched among them, must needs breed distraction of
opinions and uncertainty in that law. The like may be said of the Canon law,
albeit the Text thereof be scarce four hundred years old. But of the
professors of our law, who ever yet has made any gloss or interpretation
upon our Master Litleton? though into that little book of his, he has reduced
the principal grounds of the common law with exceeding great Judgment and
authority, and with singular Method and order? and yet if he had been an
author in the Civil or Canon law, I dare say, there had been, by this time,
so many Comments and glosses made upon him, as the books written upon this
book only, would have been more in number, then all the volumes of our law
at this day.

But the learned men in our law have ever thought, that Littleton being a
learned and reverend Judge, wrote with a purpose to be understood, and that
therefore an other man, specially if he were of less learning then he, could
hardly express him better then he has expressed himself. And therefore his
book has ever been read^ of our youngest students, without any Commentary or
interpretation at all.

But for all this, it is objected, that our later Judgments do many times
cross and contradict the former, directly, in one and the same point of law,
which is a manifest argument of incerteinty in the law.

Assuredly, there are very few precedents of such contrary Judgments, scarce
two in one age. And yet if the reasons of the later Judgments did appear of
record, we should find them grounded upon mischeefes and inconueniencies
arising since the former Judgments, or upon other weighty considerations
respecting the good of the commonwealth in general. Otherwise there are no
Judges in any State or Kingdom under the Sun, that do more reverence the
opinions and Judgments of their predecessors then the Judges of England have
ever done, as your Lordship, for their honor has observed in that most worthy
speech of the Postnati, wherein, among other things, your Lords^ip does noate
the memorable saying of Askue 37. Henr. 6 fol. 22. Such a Charter has been
allowed in the time of our predecessors, who were as sage and learned as we
be, and of Markham 4. Edw. 4. fol. 41. It is good, says he, for us to do,
as it has been used in former time, and not to keep one way one day for one
party, and another day the contrary for the other party: the former precedents
are enough for us to follow.

But on the other side, let us hear what a learned Canonist, Lodo^icus Gomez
in regula de Triennali possessore cap. 5. is bold to say, Non est inconueniens
says he iudicium esse vno tempore iustum, and postea eius contrarium iustius:
and hoc malum videtur imponi mortalibus in p^nam, vt little opiniones secundum
varietatem temporum senescant and intermoriantur, aliaeque ^iuersae vel
priorihus contrariae renascantur and deinde pubescant. Talis enim est humani
iuris disciplina, vt nulla in ^a opinio ^odem statu diu stare possit. Dies d^^i
^ructat verbum, and nox noct^ indicat scientiam.

And again, Opiniones hominum ^orum corpora sequuntur, quae cum tempore
vetara^^unt and pereunt, and s^^ut rerum omnium, ita quoque and opinionum est
quaedam vicissitudo. And in another place, Stilus hodiern^s propter ma^orem
temporum experientiam videtur magis iure fundatus, and ideo solet dic^ quod
moderni sunt sicut Culices in capite Elephantis, quae vident priora and
posteriora: quanto juniores tanto perspicatiores. And thus much may suffice to
be spoken to remove that scandal of uncertainty, which ignorance does
unworthily cast upon the common law.

2 But if the reason and ludgement of our law be so little subject to
uncertainty, how comes it to pass that the proceedings of our laws are so
much subject to delay? for this is another vulgar objection against our law
and the professors thereof. But who are they that make this objection? have
they themselves been engaged in any suites of importance? have they passed
through the Courts of Justice either in course of law, or in course of equity?
if they have not, they speak but by hearesay, and then their testimony in this
behalf is of little credit. If they have had any long depending suites of their
own, then let them examine, whither their own spleen and wilfulness, or the
corruption of some needy sollicitors, (who pick their living out of the
business they follow, and are loath to quench the fur that makes them warm)
have not rather drawn their causes to an extraordinary length, then the
ordinary process of law, or the advice of learned Counsel. For such as are
learned Counselors indeed are like good Pilots, who though their skill be
best tried in a long and difficult voyage, do rather desire fair weather, and
a speedy arrival with their passengers in the haven.

But the troth is, it is the stomach or malice of such clients as will not
stick to say, that they will spend all they are worth to have their will of
their adversaries (and therefore will not be satisfied with any Judgment or
decree) that does produce and prolong suites in law: who when their learned
Counsel indeed do refuse to nourish that peccant humor in them, do seek out
discarded impostors or Idols of whom there is an opinion among light and
ignorant people, of extraordinary cunning and slight in carrying of business
with advantage, and in curing of foild and desperate causes. These men give
them counsel according to their own heart because they sooth them in their
litigious humor: howbeit in the end when they have wearied and wasted
themselves, they find how weak those wiles and crafty courses are, and
learn of Esope, that that one only plain way which the Cart had to escape
from the dogs, was better and safer then those hundred tricks of evasion
whereof the Fox did vaunt before he was taken: and they find withal, the
saying of Cicero true, ignoratio iuris litigiosa est potius quam scientia.

Besides this malignant and unquiet disposition of many Clients, there is
another cause why suites are not brought so soon to an end as perhaps they
were in former ages: namely, the multitude of causes now depending in every
Court of justice, every of which causes must have convenient time allowed, as
well to prepare it, and make it ripe to be heard or tried, as for the trial
and hearing it self. And the true cause of the multitude of causes does
proceed from this, that the commodities of the earth being more improued, there
is more wealth, and consequently there are more contracts real, and personal,
then there were in former ages. Besides there is more luxury and excess in the
world, which breeds unthriftiness, banckruptes, and bad debtors, more
coueteousnesse and more malice, which begets force and fraud, oppression and
extortion, breath of the peace, and breach of trust. Out of these fountains
innumerable suites do spring, which make the Courts of Justice so to swell: and
hence it is that our statute laws since Henr. 8. his time, do make up so
great a volume: and hence it is, that the professors of the law are grown
withal to so great a number, for where there is magna messis there must be
of necessity operarij multi.

Indeed, if we all lived according to the law of nature, we should need
few laws, and fewer lawiers. Do as thou would be done unto were a rule
sufficient to rule us all: and every man's conscience would supply both the
place of an Advocate and a Judge, and then we should suffer no costs of suite,
nor delay of process.

And again if we were a poor and a naked people, as many nations in America
be, we should easily agree to be judged by the next man we meet, and so
make a short end of every controversy. When the people of Rome were little
better then Sheepards and heardsmen, all their laws were contained in ten or
twelve Iuory tables. But when they became Lords of all the world, what a world
of books were there written of the Roman civil law? The like we see in
every commonwealth when it once begins to flourish, and to grow rich and
mighty: the people grow proud withal, and their pride makes them contentious
and litigious, so as there is need of many laws to bridle them, and many
Officers to execute those laws, and many Lawyers to interpret those laws, and
all little enough: as when a body grows full and gross, it needs more
Physic then when it was lean.

And yet though our suites and causes be very many, and our Courts of Justice
but a few, whereby it must needs come to pass, that every particular business
moving in his turn must have the slower motion: yet if we compare our legal
proceedings with the process of other Kingdoms and commonweals, specially of
France, we shall find, that according to the vsua^l clause in diverse writts,
we have indeed plenam and celerem iustitiam (though the breefest Justice be
not always best) and that our causes for the most part being orderly pursued
may come to their Period in a year, with the course of the Sun, when there
are many processes in foreine countries that seem to be governed by Saturn,
which planet does fearce finnish his course in the space of thirty years: as
Bodin does testify of his own country, that their were more suites in law
depending in France, then in all Europe besides, and that many of those causes
were an hundred year old: as that of the county of Raise, says he, which
suite has been so well entertained in all the Chambers of Justice, as albeit
the parties that began it, are long since dead, yet the suit it self is still
alive. Besides, we have not so many Appeals, nor so many reuiewes of causes,
as the Civil and Canon laws do admit, neither have we at this day so many
delays by Essoines, viewes, vouchers, and protections, as were in use in
former ages, when titles of land were tried only in actions Real, which are
now grown almost out of use, and a more speedy course of trial invented by
m^xt and personal actions.

Lastly there is no Nation in the world (I speak it for the honor of our
Nation, and of our land) that has a course of Justice so speedy, and withal
so commodious and easy for the subject, as our trialles by Assise and nisi
prius are. For what Kingdom is there under the Sun, wherein every half
year the public Justice does make her progress into every part thereof, as
it does in the kingdoms of England and Ireland? whereby it comes to pass,
that whereas the people of other contries do travel far to seek Justice in
their fixed and settled Courts, as it were at fountains, or Cesternes, the
streams of Justice are derived unto our people, and brought by conduit pipes
or quilles even home, as it were, to their own doors. And thus much I thought
fit to observe for the clearing of that unjust imputation of long and
unnecessary delays in our legal proceedings.

3 But there is yet another exception against the professors of our law,
namely, that wittingly and willingly they take upon them the defense of many
bad causes, knowing the same to be unjust, when they are first consulted with
and retained. And this is objected by such as presume to censure our Profession
in this manner. In every cause between party and party (say they) there is a
right, and there is a wrong, yet neither the one party, nor the other did ever
want a Counselor to maintain his cause. This may be true for the most part,
and yet in truth the learned Counsel whose fortune it is to light on the wrong
side, may be free from imputation of any blame. For when does the right or
wrong in every cause appear? when is that distinguished and made manifest? can
it be discovered upon the first Commencement of the suite, and before it be
known what can be alleged and proved by either party? Assueredly it cannot:
and therefore the Counselor when he is first retained cannot possibly Judge of
the cause, whether it be just or unjust, because be hears only one part of
the matter, and that also he receaues by information from his Client, who does
ever put the case with the best advantage for himself. But when the parties
have pleaded, and are at issue: when they have examined witnesses in course of
equity, or be descended to a trial in course of law: after publication and
hearing in th^one cause, and full evidence delivered in the other: then the
learned Counsel of either side may perhaps discern the right from the
wrong, and not before. But then are the causes come to their Catastrophe, and
the Counselors Act their last part. And yet until then the true state of the
cause on both sides could not possibly be discovered.

If then the causes that are prosecuted, do for the most part hang in a
doubtful balance, until the hearing or trial thereof (for if a cause be
undoubtedly and apparently naught on the one side, no man is so unwise as to
follow it to the end, with the expense of money, and hazard of his credit) how
can it be justly said that the Counselor against whose Client a decree or
verdict does pass, has wittingly defended an unjust cause, when he wist not
how the balance would incline, until he had made his uttermost defense?
howbeit if any of our Counselors do either in the prosecution of their Clients
causes, give sinister and crafty Counsel, or upon the hearing or trial
thereof, make an over bold defense of any dishonest action, our Judges are so
tender and jealous of the honor of our profession, as they lay a noate of
Infamy upon such persons, so as they seldom or never after are permitted to
rise to any higher degree in the law, or any Office of trust in the
commonwealth.

Whereby it comes to pass, that no men of any other calling or profession
whatsoever are more careful to preserve their good name and reputation, and
stand more precisely upon their good behavior then the learned professors of
the common law.

And as our Judges do discountenance bad Counselors, so does our law abhor
the defense and maintenance of bad causes, more then any other law in the
world besides. For by what other law is unlawful maintenance, champertie, or
buying of titles, so severely punished? By what othet law does the plaintiff,
pro falso clamore, or unjust vexation, or the defendant for pleading a false
plea pay an amercement or fine to the public Justice? And this is one cause,
among others why our law does not allow Counsel unto such as are indicted of
Treason, Murder, Rape, or other capital crimes: So as never any Professor of
the law of England has been known to defend (for the matter of fact) any
Traitor, Murderer, Rauisher, or Thief, being indicted and prosecuted at the
suite of the King. Turpe reos empta miseros defendere lingua, says the Poet,
and therefore it is an honor unto our law that it does not suffer the
Professors thereof to dishonor themselves (as the Aduocats and Orators in other
countries do) by defending such offenders. For example whereof we have extant
diverse Orations of Cicero, one pro C. Rabirio perduellionis reo, another pro
Roscio Amerino who was accused of Parricide, and another pro Milone who was
accused of Murder.

But good Lawyers have not with us that liberty which good Physicians have.
For a good Physician may lawfully undertake the cure of a foul and desperate
disease, but a good Lawyer cannot honestly undertake the defense of a foul and
desperate cause. But if he fortune to be engaged in a cause, which seeming
honest in the beginning, does in the proceeding appear to be unjust, he
follows the good Counsel of the Schooleman Thom. Aquinas 22. quaest. 71.
art. 3. Aduocatus si in principio credidit causam iustam esse, quae postea in
processu appareat esse iniusta, non debet eam prodere, vt scilicet alteram
partem iuuet, reuelando causae suae secretas: Potest tamen, and debet causam
deserere, vel eum cuius causam agit, inducere ad cedendum, sieve ad componendum,
sine aduersarij da nno.

And thus I conceive, that the most common and colorable exceptions which are
taken against our law and Lawyers, may be answered and cleared by the plain
reasons and demonstrations before expressed: So as our Profession may stand and
be justified in all points against Ignorance, Envy, and ill-contented
suitors, who like choleric Chesse-plaiers, when they have had a mate given
them, could find in their hearts to cast both the Chessebord, and Chessemen
into the fire.

These vulgar errors being thus reuersed, so as we may truly say, that there
is no such uncertainty in the rules of the law, no such delay in the
proceedings, no such preuarication or corruption in the Professors thereof, as
it is by some unjustly pretended: why may we not proceed further and affirm
confidently, that the profession of the law is to be preferred before all
other humane professions and sciences, as being most noble for the matter, and
subject thereof, most necessary for the common and continual use thereof, and
most meritorious for the good effects it does produce in the common wealth?

For what is the matter and subject of our Profession but Justice, the Lady and
Queen of all moral virtues? and what are our Professors of the law but her
Counselors, her Secretaries, her Interpretors, her Servants? again, what is
the King himself but the clear fountain of Justice? and what are the
Professors of the law but conduit pipes deriuing and conueying the streams of
his Justice unto all the subjects of his several kingdoms? so as if Justice
be rightly resembled to the Sun in the firmament, in that she spreads her
light and virtue unto all creatures: how can she but communicate part of her
goodness and glory, unto that science that is her handmaid and waits upon
her? And if Kings be Gods scholars (as Homer writes) and that the rules of
Justice be their principal lesson, and if God do honor Kings with his own
name, Dixi quod dij estis (as a more divine Poet then Homer sings) specially
for that they sit upon Gods own seat when they minister Justice unto the
people, do not Kings again highly honor those persons, whose subordinate
ministry and service they use in performing that principal part of their
kingly office? Undoubtedly, touching the advancement of such persons, Solomon
the King speaks, that they shall stand before Kings, and God will set them,
says David, with Princes, even with the Princes of his people.

Neither is this Profession ennobled in regard of the dignity of her imploiment
only, but she is to be honored so much the more for the necessity and
continual use of her service in the commonweal. For if we must honor the
Physician, propter necessitatem, as the wise man prescribes: much more must
we honor, for the same cause, the professors and ministers of the law. For
neither do all men at any time, nor any one man at all times, stand in need of
the Physician: for they that are in health (which are the greatest number of
men) non egent medico, says the great Physician of our souls, and our only
Advocate which is in heaven. But all men, at all times, and in all places do
stand in need of Justice, and of law, which is the rule of Justice, and of
the interpreters and ministers of the law, which give life and motion unto
Justice.

For do not all persons stand in need of Justice, when without her rule the
Prince himself knows not how to rule, nor his people how to obey? When
without her support, the noble man cannot uphold his honor, nor the common
subject hold his liberty? When without her safeguard, the rich man cannot be
free from spoil, nor the poor man from oppression? Breefely, when without her
no man living, be he virtuous or vicious, can enjoy his life, nor any thing
that makes his life delightful? For the covetous man cannot increase his
profitt, nor the sensual man enjoy his pleasure, but under the shadow of her
wings.

Again, is not Justice needful at all times, when we can neither travel
safely by day, nor sleep securely by night with out her protection? when if
such a law were made indeed, as was propounded by a wicked Emperor, That all
laws should cease for four and twenty hours, that short cessation would be
suffitient to give opportunity to wicked men to make a greater combustion in
the whole world, then that which happened when the Chariot of the Sun did
want a guide but half a day, as it is lively expressed in the fable of Phaeton.

Lastly, is not justice necessary in all places, when we cannot without peril
make a voyage by Sea, unless she wafte us, nor a journey by land unless she
conuoy us? when we should be oppressed by force in the country, if she not
defend us, and undone by fraud in the City, if she did not relieve us? When
she encloseth every man's garden and field, and makes every man's cottage his
Castle of defense? So as we have not such an universal and continual use
neither of the light, of the Sun, nor of fire and water, as we have of the
light, and heat, and comfort of justice: for a man may remain alive some
hours without the use of those common benefitts, but a commonwealth, wherein
each private man's weal consists, cannot stand and continue one minute of an
hour, if Justice, which is her soul, be departed from her.

If therefore Justice, and the law, which is but a Rule or lesson of Justice,
be so necessary for all persons, times and places, as no family, no City, no
commonwealth, no Kingdom, can stand without the support thereof: how
needful is the service of learned men in the law without which Justice it
self cannot possibly stand? for Iustitia periret says the President
Cassanaeus, si d^esset qui iustitiam allegaret. For if no man did study the
reason of the law, if no man kept in memory the rules of the law, if no man
knew the form of pleading, or the course of proceeding in the law, what
would become of the public Justice in a short time? or how should the benefitt
of the law be derived and communicated unto the people? for as in a natural
body the reasonable soul cannot use or transmitt any of her powers, but by
special Organs of the same body disposed and fitted by nature for every
function, as the eye to see, the ear to hear, the tongue to speak, and the
like of the rest: so in the body politic of a common wealth, the law which is
the soul thereof, produces no effect or operation at all, but by such of her
ministers, as by art and experience are enabled and qualified for her service.
For lex est iustitia inanimata, says the schoolman, Lex est mutus magistratus
says Cicero: the law of it self is dumb and speaks not, but by the tongue
of a learned and eloquent Lawyer: she is deaf and hears no complaints, but
by the ear of a grave and patient Judge: she is blind and sees no
enormities, but by the eye of a watchful and diligent Officer.

Again, the law is nothing else but a Rule, that is made to measure the
actions of men. But a Rule is dead and measures nothing, unless the hand of
the Architect do apply it. It is indeed an excellent Instrument, to make
harmony and concord in the commonwealth: but the best Lute that ever was made,
could never make music of it self alone, without the learned hand of the
Luteplaier.

Therefore though Iuppiter (as Protagoras in Plato tells us) did first invent
and give the law, yet was Mercury sent with that heavenly gift, to deliver it
ever unto mankind. So as it is manifest, that without the ministry of these
Mercuries, of these interpretors of the law, namely the learned Professors
thereof, there can be no use or application of the law, and consequently the
law or Justice it self cannot consist without them.

But as the estimation and price of this Profession is exceedingly raised by
the necessary and universal use thereof, so do her great merits to the
commonwealth deserve a far greater exaltation of honor. For first the
commonwealth is indebted unto the law for all her temporal blessings and
felicities whatsoever. For all our peace, plenty, ciuility, and moral honesty
depends upon the law. That we enjoy our lives, our wives, our children,
our lands, our goods, our good names, or whatsoever is sweet and dear unto
us, we are beholding to the law for it. Quid sunt regna nisi latrocinia sine
iustitia? says Saint Augustine? without Justice the land would be full of
thieves, the Sea full of Pirates, the Commons would rise against the Nobility,
the Nobility against the Crown: we should not know what were our own, what
another man's, what we should have from our Ancestors, what we should leave
to our Children: Maios haereditas venit vnicuique nostrum a Iure and legibus,
quam a parentibus says Cicero: in a word there would be nothing certain,
nothing sure, no contracts, no commerce, no conversation among men, but all
Kingdoms, and states would be brought to confusion, and all humane society
would be dissolved.

But on the other side, the law is a fortress for the weak to retire unto, a
Sanctuary for the oppressed to fly unto, it restrains the boldness of the
insolent, it tieth with Manacles the hands of the potent, and like Orpheus
harp or Noahes Arc, it charmeth the fiercenes of the Lion and the Tiger, so
as the poor lamb may ly in safety by them.

If then the law it self does merit so highly of all mankind in general,
for that it is the fountain of all these benefitts, what do the Professors of
the law deserve, which draw these benefitts out of that fountain, and derive
the same unto every particular person? Nam si non habes quo haurias, and puteus
altus est, as the woman of Samaria says of Jacobs well in the Gospel, how
can thou refresh thy self with the water of that well? Si veritas sit in
profundo demersa, as Democritus was wont to say, if humane actions be so
carried in clouds, as it is hard to find what is true and what is false, and
when the truth of the fact is found, it is many times as hard to distinguish
what is just and what is unjust, if in all causes that come in question, either
quaestio facti or question Iuris must first be decided, before a man can
receive the benefitt of the law (for as the wise man says, Deus fecit
hominem rectum, sed ipse miscuit se infinitis quaestionibus) what a meritorious
work is it, to resolve these troublesome questions which arise in the Civil
life of man, either by laying open the truth of the fact, or by clearing the
doubtful point in law, that speedy and equal Justice may be done unto all,
and every one may have and enjoy his own in peace? how often would the truth
be concealed and suppressed? How often would fraud ly hid and undiscovered? How
many times would wrong escape and pass unpunished, but for the wisdom and
diligence of the Professors of the law? Does not this profession every day
comfort such as are grieved, counsel such as are perplexed, relieve such as
are circumuented, prevent the ruin of the improuident, save the innocent,
support the impotent, take the Pray out of the mouth of the oppressor, protect
the Orphan, the widow, and the stranger? Is she not Oculus caeco, and pes
claudo as Job speaks? Does she not withal many times stretch forth brachium
saculare in defense of the Church and true Religion? All which are works of
mercy, and of singular merit. Again, does she not register and keep in
memory ^he best Antiquities of our Nation? Does she not preserve our ancient
Customs and form of government, wherein the wisdom of our Ancestors does
shine far above the policy of other kingdoms? Are not the Records of her
acts and proceedings so precious, as they are kept in the Kings Treasury,
like Iewelles of the Crown, and reputed a principal part of the Royal
treasure? Lastly, is not a worthy Prosessor of the law a starr in the
firmament of the commonwealth? Is he not Lux in tenebris wheresoever he
dwells? Is not his house as it were an Oracle not only to a Town or City,
but to a whole country round about him? So as he may truly say of the people
that seek his counsel, as Apollo Pithius spoke in Ennius of such as resorted
unto his Temple. Suarum rerum incerti, quos ego open mea
Ex incertis Certos, compotesque consilij
De^itto, ne res temere tractent turbidas.


Therefore one of the Roman Emperors does not without cause give this
honorable testimony of the Professors of the law, Aduocati, qui dirimunt
ambigua facta causarum, suaeque desensionis viribus, tam publicis in rebus quam
in priuatis, lapsa erigunt, satigata reficiunt, non minus human^ generi
provident, quam si praelijs atque vulneribus patriam parentesque saluarent:
neque enim solos nostro Imperio militare credimus, qui gladijs, elypeis, and
Thoracibus nituntur, sed etiam aduocatos. Militant namque causarum Patroni, qui
laborantium spem, vitam, and posteros defendunt. For if it be a worthy deed
(as doubtless it is) for a man to defend his freindes or country with his
right hand and his sword only: what an excellent service is it to defend them
with his speech, his reason, and wisdom wherein the excellency of man does
principally consist.

Therefore both the Schooleman and the Politic do prefer Justice before
Fortitude, and the Statute of 31. Henr. 8. cap. 10. which ranketh the great
Officers of the kingdom in their due places, does place the Constable and
Marshall beneath the Chauncellor in all assemblies of Counsel. For Ille semel
(says Cicero) speaking of the Martial man, Hic semper proderit Reipublicae,
meaning the learned man of the long robe.

And in very troth, as the commonwealth is much beholding to the Profession of
the law, so are the Professors of the law not a little beholding to the
commonwealth. For if they procure and preserve her peace and her plenty, does
not she requite them again with riches and with honor? Does she not advance
them to her chief Benches and Offices, and trust them with the liuelyhoode and
lives of all her people? Neither do our learned men of the law grow to good
estates in the commonwealth, by any illiberall means (as envy sometime
suggesteth) but in a most ingenous and worthy manner. For the fees or rewards
which they receive are not of the nature of wages, or pay, or that which we
call salery or hire, which are indeed duties certain, and grow due by
contract for labor or service, but that which is given to a learned
Counselor, is called honorarium and not merces, being indeed a gift which
gives honor as well to the Taker as to the giver: neither is it certain or
contracted for, for no price or rate can be set upon Counsel, which is
invaluable and inestimable, so as it is more or less according to
circumstances, namely the ability of the Client, the worthiness of the
Counselor, the weightynesse of the cause, and the custom of ^he Country.
Breefely, it is a gift of such a nature, and given and taken upon such
terms, as albeit the able Client may not neglect to give it, without noate of
ingratitude (for it is but a gratuity or token of thankefullnesse) yet the
worthy Counselor may not demand it, without doing wrong to his reputation:
according unto that moral rule, Multa honest accipi possunt, quae tamen
honest peti non possunt.

Lastly, it is an infallible argument, that the estates of such as rise by the
law, are builded upon the foundation of virtue, in that Gods blessing is so
manifestly upon them, not only in raising, but in preserving their houses and
posterities: whereof there are examples not a few, and those not obscure, in
every shire of England, and of the English Pale in this kingdom of Ireland.

If then our common law of England be clear from those vulgar imputations,
which ignorance does conceive, and envy report thereof, if the Profession or
science of the law be more noble, more necessary, more meritorious then any
other temporal Art or Science: and if the dignity of this profession do
accordingly dignifie all the Professors thereof which are qualified with
learning and virtue fit for so worthy a Calling (for such as are ignorant or
dishonest, as they are to receive no grace by the Profession, so the profession
is to suffer no disgrace by them) how highly is that person honored, whose true
merit has advanced him to the most transcendent place of honor, that can
possibly be attained by that Profession? This is that great place or Office
which your Lordship most worthily holds (and long may you hold the same)
under his Majesty, which though it be the highest pinnacle of honor, that any
secular person of the long Robe can in re or spe aspire to, under any Monarch,
yet was it given unto your Lordship, nec expetenti, nec expectanti, by the
wisest and most renowned Queen that ever reigned in Europe: who leaving her
Crown together with the faithful servants thereof unto the wisest and Iustest
King upon earth, his Majesty in his infallible Judgment and wisdom
confirmed this honorable Office unto your Lordship, not only with a fullness
of grace to your selse, but with an augmentation of honor to your posterity.
For what increase of honor can the Lord Chauncellor receive in his own person,
being that in England which Joseph was in Egypt, the second person of the
Realm in the administration of all Civil affairs? being made (as it were)
chief Steward of his masters house, the chief dispenser of his bounty and
Justice, by the delivery and custody of the great Seal, which may properly
be called the key of the Kingdom? Being the Sole Judge of that high Court,
which is Seeds misericordie, and therefore exalted above all seats of Justice:
where he has Potestatem absolutam as well as regulatam in binding and loosing
the proceedings of the law, and in deciding of causes by the rules of his own
conscience.

Breefely what can there be more done to the man whom the King will honor? Is
he not ad latus Principis, to attend him? Is he not Auricularius Principis to
advise him? Does not the King make him a conduit of his wisdom, when he
uses his voice and tongue to declare his Royal pleasure? And does he not make
him an Organ of his goodness, when he trusts him with his mercy and
conscience, in sweetening the bitter waters of Summum ius, and in mittigating
the rigor of the law unto his people? In a word does he not represent
reuerentiam Principis in the power and authority of his Office? And do not the
people fear and honor the King even in the gravity and dignity of his person?
And are not all these honors made more honorable, and exceedingly raised in
true estimation and value, when the same are enjoyed in a most famou^ and
flourishing commonwealth, and do proceed as Sun beams from the most
religious, learned, wise, the most renowned and excellent King of the world? If
then the greatest honors do of right belong to the greatest virtues (for what
is honor but a reflection and reward of virtue?) how virtuous a person must he
be, with what gifts and graces, with what abilities, and ornaments, both of
art and nature must he be endowed, who can worthily supply that great and
honorable Office?

Assuredly, besides the natural faculties and powers of his mind which he
ought to have in great perfection, and besides the outward comeliness and
dignity of his person, for Gratior est pulchro veniens (...) corpore virtus,
and Sapientia hominis lucet in vul^ueius, says Solomon, he must be furnished
withal learning that has any relation to the public good: Divinity, Law,
Policy, Morality, and specially Eloquence, to impart and communicate all the
rest. He must withal have a long and universal experience in all the
affairs of the commonwealth: he must be accomplished and absolute in all
points of Gravity, Constancy, Wisdom, Temperance, Courage, Justice, Piety,
Integrity, and all other virtues fit for Magistracy and government, yet so, as
the same be seasoned and tempered with affability, gentleness, humanity,
courtesy, howbeit without descending or diminishing of himself, but still
retaining his dignity, state, and honor. Breefely, he must be a person of
such virtue and worthiness, as his life may be a Censure, and his example a
Mirror for all other Magistrates. These are the excellencies and perfections
wherewith that great Officer must be qualified and adorned: And this Idaea
have I conceived of him, not out of mine own imagination or weak discourse of
reason, but out of an humble observation of your Lordship in whom not only
those abilities and virtues before expressed, but many other graces and
ornaments do shine so brightly, as the weakest Judgment may collect out of the
same a most exquisite Pattern of a most excellent Chauncellor.

But perhaps it would be thought more comely for me to pronounce this of
your Lordship to others collaterally, then to speak it to your Lordship of
your self directly (though what can I declare in this kind to others, but
that which the world has long since known and acknowledged?) yet is there no
man living whom it may better become then my self to praise and honor your
Lordship every way, whose favor has been (as it were) a good Angel unto me,
and to whom I stand bound for so many benefits, as that which might carry a
show of adulation in another, must needs be thought but Duty and gratitude
in me.

Howbeit, besides my particular obligation, there is no Professor of the law,
that is not obliged to do your Lordship all honor, for the honor you have done
to the Profession of the law, whereof your Lordship has been, during all
your time, a principal light and ornament.

And now (my most honorable good Lord) my devotion to the Profession of the
law, and to your Lordship the most noble Patron of the Professors thereof,
having enlarged this discourse beyond the measure and limitts of an Epistle
(and therefore I give it the name of a Preface) it remains that I present
unto your Lordship the ^ude collection of a few selected Cases, which since
the beginning of his Majesty's Reign have been argued, resolved, and adjudged
in this Realm of Ireland.

These are the first fruits of my labor in this kind of learning, and are
therefore a due and proper oblation to your Lordship, for that my study have
yealded the better fruit, being cherished by the Sunnebeames of your Lordships
favor. This is also the first Report of Cases arising in Ireland and ruled in
the Courts of Justice there, that ever was made and published to the world,
since the laws of England were first established in this kingdom. Lastly, I
have made choice of such special Cases, as are either proper for this kingdom
only, or else do contain for the most part points of learning not common, or
at least not largely debated, in our books of the law.

But for as much as Natura incipit ab imperfectis as the Schooleman says,
Therefore these weak and imperfect beginnings do seek your Lordships
protection, till time shall give them more strength and reputation. In the
mean time, if your Lordships Judgment shall allow the publication hereof, I
shall have the less cause to doubt the censure of any other, specially of my
Masters of the law in England, if any of these books happen to come to their
hands: to whom I may truly make this protestation, that these Cases being
resolved and adjudged in the Courts of Justice in Ireland, are not collected
and published by me, to increase the number of the books of law in England,
or to interrupt the better studies of the Students there, by reading of this
collection, but principally for the use and benefitt of our practisers here in
Ireland, and to move and incite others in this Kingdom, by this first example,
which does only open and show them the way, to perform the like service
hereafter to posterity.

Neither have I besought your Lordships patronage for these Cases of Ireland in
respect of my self only, but also in regard of that relation which your
Lordsh^p has unto this kingdom. For albeit pour Lordship be Lord Chauncellor
of England, yet the great Seal^ which you keep there, is also of force and
power within this Realm. Neither can we forgett, without ingratitude, that
your Lorship in that high place of Counsel which you hold in England, is upon
all occasions watchful and careful of the public good and welfare of Ireland.

Now therefore the only wise God who has given unto your Lordship those
blessings which wisdom has in store for them that love her, Longitudo dierum
in dextra ^ius, and in sinistra ^ius diuitiae and gloria, preserve your
Lordship for many years in health and honor, that you may long continue a
prudent and principal Counselor unto your Sovereign, a loving and provident
father unto your country, a comfort and countenance to your particular
freindes, and all others who partake of your honorable favors, among which I
remain.

Most bound, and most devoted to do your Lordship all humble service. Io: Dauys.

Trin. 2. Iacobi en ^^eschecquer.
Le Case de Proxies.
ENter le Roy and Sir Ambrose Forth, Doctor del civil Ley, and un des Masters
del Chauncery, le case fuit tiel.

L'Evesque de Meth, devantle dissolution des Monasteries, avoit un Proxy de
xv. (...). iiii. d. payable annualment horse del co^maund^y de Kells en le
county de Meth, parcel del possessions del Hospital de Saint John de Jerusalem
en Ireland; and un auter Proxy de xx s. paiable annualme^t horse del
impropriate Rectory de Trevet en mesme le county, parcel del possessions del
Abbey de Thomascourt en le county de Dublin.

Anno 33. Henr. 8. le dit Hospital de S. John de Jerusalem, and le dit Abbey
de Thomascourt fueront suppress and dissol^e, and touts les possessions des
ambideux ditz measons fueront ^est en le actual and real possession del Corone
per act del Parliament. Mes en mesme le act est un express Saving de Proxies
a^ touts Evesques and lour successors.

Apres ceo, l'Evesque de Meth and son Clergy, (car cest evesquery ^ad Deane
and Chapter) per fait enroll dat: 16. Martij 36. H. 8 grant les Proxies
a^antdit (enter auters) al Roy Henr. 8. ses heirs and successors; le dit Roy
esteant al temps del grant and puis, en actual possession del dit co^maundry
and Rectory, horse de queux les ditz Proxies fueront payable.

Puis, la Run Elizabeth, per ses letters pate^ts dat 1. Novembris en l'an.
33. de sa reign, demise le dit commaundry et Rectory al Doctor Forth, rendant
rent, sa^s ascun reservation des Proxies. Et si ore il serra charge o^e les
dits Proxies, and les arrerages d'yceux encurrues depuis le comenceme^t de son
leas, fuit le question. Et fuit adiudge que il serroit charge. Et 3. points
fuero^t move, and debate en l'argume^t de cest Case. 1 Si les Proxies fueront
tout ousterment extinct per le suppression and dissolution des dits religious
measons de S. John de Jerusalem, and de Thomascourt, nient obstant le dit
Saving, deins l'act de Dissolution. 2 Si l'Evesque puissoit graunter les
Proxies al Roy. 3 Si les Proxies fueront extinct en maines del Roy per l'Unity
de possession.


PUr le Primer point, fuit object per le counsel del Sir Ambrose Forth, que les
Proxies fueront extinct per le suppression and dissolution de les religious
measons: pur ceo que le Visitatio^ de les religious measons fuit la sole cause
de payment des Proxies. Et cessante causa cessat effectus. Car les religious
persons esteant deraigne, and disperse, ne fueront, apres ceo, subject al
visitation, and donques, quant le visitation cease, le Proxy, esteant solement
un Exhibition doneal visitor purses travayling charges, cessera auxi. Car
Procuratio (come les Canonists define ceo) est exhibitio sumptuum necessariorum
facta praelatis, qui diocoeses peragrando Ecclesias subiectas visitant.

Uncore ils agreeont, que le Visitation ne cessa immediatement per le
surrender, ouper l'act de Parliament, que done les religious measons and lour
possessions al Corone; car per ceo, lour Corporations ne fueront dissolve; come
est tenus en le case de Deane and Chapter de Norwich en le 3. part des Reports
de le Seignior Coke. 15. Ass. p. 8. 32. H. 8 Br. Corporations 78. mes quant les
religious persons fueront deraigne, and avoent relinquish lour habit, rule, and
order, pur queux ils fueront visitable, adonques le Corporation fuit ousterment
dissolve, and sur ceo le visitation cessa.

Et ils reseinblont le proxy due pur Visitation al Annuity pro Consilio, ou
pro Servitio impe^dendo; si le counsel, ou le service soit withdraw, le
Annuity est determine: issint si Rent charge soit grant pur un chimin, stopp le
chimin, and le Rent charge serra auxi stopp. 9. Edw. 4. 19. 15. Edw. 4. 2. 21.
Edw. 3. 7. 45. Ed. 3. 8. Dier. 6. H. 8. 2. and 6. Ed. 6. 76. issint ou Corody
est grant pur certain service destre fait; omission del service, determine le
Corody. 20. Edw. 4. fol. vltimo.

Fuit auxi dit, que cest duty ne fuit annual, mes co^tingent; and payable
tantsolement sur chescun Visitation, come Escuage sur chescum journey royal,
ou come Aid pur ^le marier, on pur fair fitz chivaler; en queux darrain cases,
si avowry soit fait pur l'ayde, est bon plea en bar d'avowry, a dire, que
l'avowant and teil fitz ou ^ile en vie, al temps del Aid levy. N. N. Br. 82.
g.

Et pur le Saving, ils diont, que ceo fuit un Flattring Saving, que ne poer
preserver les proxies en esse, queux la Ley ad extinguish, come est tenus 14.
Eliz. Dier. 313. que les Tenures del Obitou Chauntry terres tenus del subjects,
sont extinct per lact del 1, E. 6. meant obstant le Saving en le dit act,
propter absurditatem; issint les Proxies icy serront extinct propter
absurditate^. Car come est absurd que le Roy serra subject al attendance en
respect de Tenute, issint est absurd, que le Roy serra subject al visitation ou
al ascun duty en respect de ceo. De mesme le nature sont plusors Savings mise
en Walsinghams case, Plow. Com. 563. queux so^t la appell Flattring Savings.

PUr le second point, fuit object, que l'Evesque ne puissoit graunter les
proxies al Roy, pur: 2. reasons: l'u^ deduce del person del Roy, l'auter del
person del Evesque. 1. pur le Roy, admit que il ^uit capable de teil
spiritual office, come destre Visitor des religious persons, vncor il navera
Poxies pur l'inconveniency, and indecency, and auxi pur l'impossibility. Car
nest convenient, ou decent, que les poures religious persons porteront les
charges de Roy, and est auxi impossible. Car per le Canon Ley, Procuratio
exhibe^da est secundum qualitatem personae visitantis; and le Majesty del
person del Roy, and le grandeur de son Train est teil, que per presumption del
ley, null private person poet porter ses necessary charges, ou fair a luy
entertaynment aggreeable al quality de son person. Et pur ceo est tenus 27.
Henr. 8. 10 b que ou common sans number fuit grant al Abbe and ses successors
horse del Manor de Dale, que apres le dissolution, le Roy na^era le common sans
number: car la est dit, que si le Roy ave ^oit, il puissoit surcharge la terre,
que la ley ne voet suffer. Come si un villain purchase common sans number, le
Seignior navera ceo, pur le reason avantdi^, car donques le ter^^nant perdroit
la profitt de sa terre.

Pur l'Evesque, coment que il poet graunter ses Temporal Possessions ove
l'assent de son Chapter ou Clergy: uncore ceux duties queux il ad per
prerogative de ^on Episcopal Chair, ou come incident a son spiritual
function, il ne poet graunter: queux per la rule del Cahon ley sout de 3.
Kinds. 1. Subsidium Cathedratieum, que est duty de prerogative and
superiority. 2. quarta Episcopalis, que est done a luy pur rep^ration de les
Esglises. 3. Procurationes, pur son visitation, vt supra, que est perquisite ou
profit de son spiritual jurisdiction. Come creation money done al Duke, ou
Earl, pur maintenance de ^on honor, ne poet estre grant ouster. 6. Henr. 8.
Dier. 2. a.

PUrle 3. point, ils diont, que coment que Proxy soit un personal chose,
payable tantsolement en respect des persons Visitable: vncor admit que ceux
Proxies sont devenus real, and que le commaundry and rectory sont charges ove
ceux Proxies, donques le unity de possession extinguish euxen Maines del Roy:
come Seigniory, rent charge, common, and similia sont extinguish per le
purchase del Terrtenant, sil ad equal estate en le terre, and en la chose que
charge la terre. Et a cest intent le case de 2. H 4. 19. a. fuit mise, ou un
Prior an annuity ho^s* del personage per Prescription, le personage est
appropriate al Priory, l'annuity est extinguish a touts iours.

MEs d'auter part, fuit respond per le counsel del Roy, and resolve per le
Court, que les dits Proxies ne fueront extinguish per le dissolution des dits
religious measous, mes fueront bien preserve and saves al Evesque: Et que
l'evesque ad eux bien grant al Roy: and que le unity de posses on en maines
del Roy fesoit forsque un suspension, and nemi extinguishment des dits Proxies.

QUant al Primer point, primerment fuit observe, que les Proxies n'avoent lour
Original en le Primitive Church. Car Saint Paul en visitant touts les
Esglises queux il ad plant en Asia and Europe, ne demaundoit ascun Proxies, mes
laboroit ove ses proper maines, pur son sustenance, ne serroit burdensome al
Esglises. Uncor long temps apres, le Canon ley, que declare que Proxies sont
due al Euesques en lour Visitations, dit, que ceo est agreeable al doctrine de
S. Paul, vt a quibus spiritualia recipimus, eisdem Temporalia co^municemus.
Iustit. Iuris Canon libr 2. c. deCe^sib.

Secondment fuit note, que ceo que nous appellons Proxy ou procuracy, est
appell per les Canonists, Procuratio, pur ceo que sur chescun visitation les
persons visitable procuront necessary provisions pur les visitors: queux
prouisio^s al primes fueront fait en refections ou victuals, viz. in
esculentis and Poculentis, mes ceo fuit ove measure and Te^perance, ne
ieiunioru^ doctrina^ rube^tibus buccis praedice^t, come est dit en vndes Canons

Mes apres, quant le pomp and excess des visitors requiroit tant des
provisions que fueront grievous and intolerable al Esglises, and religious
measons: adonques chescun Esglise and meason fuit reasonablement tax, and per
ceo^, chescun Proxy fuit reduce al certain sum de money, payable annualment,
en nature de pension, al Ordinary, que ad le power de visitation de mero Iure,
come est dit. 10. Eliz. Dier. 273. b.

Et ceo fuit aptement resemble al Socage en nostre Ley, de que Littleton dit,
que en ancient temps, devant le limitation de Temps de memory, grand part de
les Tenants queux tiendront de lou^ Seigniors per Socage, devoent vener ove
lour Sokes per certain iours per an, pur arer and semer les demesnes del
Seignior; and pur ceo que tiels overages fueront fait pur le viver and
sustenance de lour Seigniors, ils fueront quites envers lour Seigniors de touts
auters services; and puis apres tiels services fuetout changes en Deniers, per
consent des Seigniors, and per desire des tenants, en un Annuel Rent* etc. Et
sicome les tenants en Socage apres le dit change, payerent lour Rents
annualment al Seignior, coment que il ad alien ses Demesnes, and ad null terre
destre Are ou seem: issint les Esglises and Religious measons, apres que le
procuration de victuals fuit reduce al sum certain, payeront ceo al Ordinary
annualment, coment que il ne fesoit ascun Visitation. Et issint le Rule de
Cessante causa cessat effectus, ne tient en ceux Cases. Et a cest intent
directment est le case de Sir William Capel, mise en Luttrels Case, en le. 4.
part. des Reports de le Seignior Coke, ou fuit resolve: que ou home tient
certain terre per Rent pur Castle Guard, coment que le Castle soit dirute o^
decay, vncor le rent remain; Car la est dit, que quant le Tenant regnoit del
Seignior, de garder, ou repair le Castle del Seignior, and puis teil service
(come Littleton dit en Case de Socage) fuit en ancient temps, per mutual
consent del Seignior and tenant, change en un Annuel Rent; coment que soit dit
rent Pro warda Castri, vncor le Seignior ne poet aver le Castle Guard arrere,
quant il voet, mes, apres le composition and change fait, le Castle Guard est
tout ousterment ale; and Pro ne import condition, come en le case d'annuity
grant Pro consilio impendendo, mes plein and perpetuel recompense and
satsfaction. Pur mesme le reason en nostre case, coment que les personages
impropriate sont fait ore lay fee, and devenus en maines des Lays gents, queux
ne sont visitable; and coment que les religious measons sont suppress,
dissolve, and dirute, come le Castle en Sir William Capels Case; vncor les dits
certain sums de money queux veignont en liew de Proxies, and retaynont le
nosme de Proxies, and per ancient composition sont fait parcel del certain and
settled revenewes del Evesque, remaineront a touts iours, and ne serront subject
al extinguishment; nient plus que Annuites, pensions, ou portions de Tithes,
queux sont pay a cest iour horse del plusors Abbeyes, and impropriate Rectories,
and l'originall causes, pur queux ils fueront grount ou pay, ne serront ore
examine, ou brought en question. Et a cest iour le Roy mesme pay and allow
Proxies horse de touts impropriations queux il ad en son possession; and put ceo
en quescun lease fait per le Roy del impropriate rectory, il y ad un ^ovenant
del part del lessee, que il portera and payera touts Proxies, Synodalls,
pensions, etc. Et Sir Humfrey Winch adonques cheef Baron disoit, que devant le
dissolution des Monasteries, ou rectory fuit appropriate al Abbey,
immediatement le Visitation cessoit quant ^l Rectory; car l'Abbe ne fuit
visitable come Rector, pur son doctrine, mes come Abbe, pur son rule and order:
and vncor sans question le Ordinary ad ses Proxies horse de touts personages
appropriate al Abbeyes, cibien devant le Dissolution, que apres.

Et pur le Saving en l'act de 33. H. 8. c. 5. ceo nest idle, ou Flattring
Saving, mes real and effectual; car est agree devant, que ceux Proxies sueront
en esse all temps del fesans del Act, and nient extinguish per le Surrender des
religious measons, car lour Corporations ne fueront dissolve, tant que les
religious persons ont relinquish lour measons, and ont estre disperse: Et
donques tiels chooses, queux fueront en esse al temps del fesans del act, poent
estre bien preserve, and save per l'act, coment que chooses extinct devant, ne
poent estre revive per un Saviug, sans express parolx de grant and
restitution. Et cest difference appiect pleinment en le case de Kekewich. 27.
H. 8. Brook Parliaments 77. Et en Sir John Molins Case en le. 6. part. des
Reports de le Seignior Coke.

2 QUant al Second point, fuit resolve, que les Proxies en lour Original
nature esteant duties payable pur Visitation, fueront grauntable al Roy, and le
Roy fuit capable de tiel grant, specialment, quant les dits duties fueront
convert al Sum de money certain, en nature de pension ou Annuity. Car per le
ancient Ley del Realm, le Roy ad power de visiter, reformer, and correcter
touts abuses and enormities en l'Esglise: and per le statutes fait en temps H.
8. le Corone fuit forsque remitt and restore a son ancient jurisdiction; que
fuit usurp per L'evesque de Rome. 33. E. 3. fitz. Aid del Roy. 103. Reges
sacro oleo vncti spiritualis iurisdictionis sunt capaces. Et Proxy est profit
del Irisdiction. 10 H. 7. 18. Rex est mixta persona cum Saccrdote. Auxi le Roy
avera Tithes per le common ley, de queux null lay person fuit capable. 22. Ass.
p. 75 21. H. 7. 1. Le Roy mesme visitera ses frank Chapels, and Hospitalls. 8.
Ass. p. 29. N. Br. 42. a. Et Cassaneus in Catalogo Gloriae mundi part. 5.
consideratione. 24. Cite un text del Canon Ley, Viz. quod omnes reges dicuntur
Clerici, Et un auter text, que dit, Quod causa spiritualis committi potest
Principi laico.

Et ou fuit dit, que en respect del grandeur del Roy, and de son train,
competent Proxies ne poent estre exhibit a luy, and per consequence, ne poent
estre grauut a luy; test objection est toll, en ceo que les Proxies al temps de
cest grant fueront reduce al certain reasonable sums de money: Auxi le rule
del Canon Ley nest pleiriment cite devant, car le rule est. Procuratio
exhibenda est secundum qualitatem personae visitantis, and substantiam
visitatorum.

Fuit auxi resolve, que L'evesque, ove lassent de son Clergy, puissoit bien
graunter les Proxies al Roy, pur ceo que la Ley ad qualify le person del Roy
de receaver teil grant, coment que ceo ^oit tiel prerogative del Evesque que
ne poet esire assign al auter person: come le creation money del Duke ou
Countee, poet estre grant and surrender al Roy, coment que ne poet estre
grant al subject. Auxi les Proxies esteant ore reduce al certain sum de
Argent, and issint ^air part del certain, settled, and perpetual revenew del
Evesque, poent estre cibien grant per luy, come portion de Tithes, ou Annuity,
ou ascun de ses Rents, services, ou auters hereditaments temporal.

3 QUant al Tierce point, fuit auxi resolve and adindge, que le unity de
possession des Proxies ove les rectories impropriate and religious measons,
horse de queux les Proxies sont payable, ne Extinguish les Proxies en maines del
Roy, mes Suspend le Payment de eux Tantum, pro tempore, quovsque le Roy per son
grant ad sever l'nn del auter.

Pur trover la Ley en cest point, le nature de ceux chooses fuit consider, queux
en ascun manner poent estre subject al Drowning, ou Extinguishment, per Unity
de possession. Et ceux sont de 3. Kinds. 1. Estates en terre. 2. Actions Real
and Titles al terre. 3. chooses issuant ou provenant horse del Terre, ou prises
and eu sur terre.

1 E^^ates en terre, sont properment merge ou confound, quant meinder estate
concur ove greinder estate, en mesme le person, and en mesme le droit: come,
Terminus and feodum non possunt constare simul in vna eademque persona. Et le
pregnant reason de ceo est mise en le case de Bracebridge Plow. Com. 319. b.
Pur ceo que Term est temps finite, and Fee simple est temps infinite, and le
finite de necessity covient estre merge and confound en le infinite. Mes ceo
est Confounding and nemi Extinguishment: car si particular tenant grant ou
surrender son estate al cesty en reversion, le particular estate nest extinct,
coment que soit merge and confound: car si particular tenant ad charge le Terre
ove rent, ou anter encumbrance, l'Estate remainera en essea cest intent. Et
revera Estates en terre, and Droit al terre, sont chooses de tiel Substance,
queux ne poent estre extinct, ou interire interire penitus. Et pur ceo
Littleton dit, que si disseisee, quant son entry est toll, relessa al
terrtenant tout son droit, que ceo ne envrera per voy de Extinguishment; car le
droit que il avoit passa al tenant per son Releas: Et serroit inconvenient, que
tiel ancient droit serroit extinct tout ousterment, Car droit ne poet pas
morier.

Mes chooses queux issuont horse del terre, come Seignory, rent charge, etc. sout
subject al Extinguishment a touts entents. Come (...) Seignior grant son
Seigniory al Tertenant and estrange, la serra null ioyntenancy, ou survivor
enter eux, carle moity del Seigniory est extinct a touts entents and purposes.
